When the vertex set V is parti-tioned into disjoint color groups of equal size, but the number of colors |χ| is arbitrary (not neces-sarily a power of two), we design an algorithm called fair-equi . The algorithm fair-equi builds upon two subroutines: fairpower-of-two and make-pdc-fair . The subroutine fairpower-of-two 

is already described. Let us first describe the high-level idea of make-pdc-fair and then present the construction of fair-equi .

High-Level Idea of make-pdc-fair . The subroutine make-pdc-fair takes as input a clustering 

I = {I1, I 2, . . . , I η} over a vertex set V whose points are colored from a color set ζ = {z1, z 2, . . . , z r}.The total number of points of each color satisfies a prescribed global proportion 

z1(V ) : z2(V ) : . . . : zr(V ) = p1 : p2 : . . . : pr,

where, without loss of generality, p1 > p 2 > · · · > p r. Furthermore, each cluster Ii in I is assumed to be p-divisible —that is, the number of points of each color zj in Ii is an integer multiple of pj .The goal of make-pdc-fair is to transform I into a clustering Fmpf such that every cluster 

F ∈ F mpf satisfies 

z1(F ) : z2(F ) : . . . : zr(F ) = p1 : p2 : . . . : pr.

The algorithm proceeds in ⌈log 2 r⌉ iterations, progressively aligning the local color proportions toward the global target ratio. Starting from F0 := I, it maintains a sequence of refined clusterings 

I = F0 → F 1 → · · · → F ⌈log 2 r⌉ = Fmpf .

In each iteration t, make-pdc-fair merges adjacent color blocks in a hierarchical fashion. Initially, each color forms a singleton block B0 

> j

= {zj }; at every subsequent iteration t ≥ 1, consecutive blocks are merged in pairs: 

Bti = Bt−12i−1 ∪ Bt−12i , for i = 1 , 2, . . . , 

j mt−1

2

k

.

If the number of blocks mt−1 is odd, the final block remains unpaired and is carried forward unchanged. This process continues until all colors are contained in a single block at iteration 

T = ⌈log 2 r⌉.At every iteration t, the algorithm ensures that all colors within each block Bti appear in every cluster in proportion to their prescribed global ratios. To achieve this, make-pdc-fair compares the scaling factors of two adjacent blocks Bt−12i−1 and Bt−12i within each cluster and tries to equalize the scaling factor. To understand how make-pdc-fair equalizes scaling factors, recall that after iteration (t − 1) , every cluster F t−1 satisfies the correct color ratios within each block Bt−12i−1 and 

Bt−12i separately. In particular, for 

Bt−12i−1 = {za1 , . . . , z as } and Bt−12i = {zb1 , . . . , z bu },

we can write the color counts of zaj and zbk for j ∈ [1 , s ] and k ∈ [1 , u ] respectively inside F t−1 as 

zaj (F t−1) = paj · x, zbk (F t−1) = pbk · y, 

where the integers x and y are the scaling factors of the two blocks. The values x and y indicate how many times the global ratio vector (pa1 , . . . , p as ) or (pb1 , . . . , p bu ) is realized inside the cluster. 26 10 c1

Figure 1.5: Visualization of the make-pdc-fair algorithm for three colors {c1, c 2, c 3}, with 20 , 12 ,and 8 vertices respectively, corresponding to a global ratio of 5 : 3 : 2 . In the first iteration, the color blocks are B11 = {c1, c 2} and B12 = {c3}. Hence, in the intermediate clustering F1, each cluster maintains the local ratio c1 : c2 = 5 : 3 . The scaling factor of block B11 in cluster F 11 is x = 2 , while that of B12 is y = 3 . To align these scaling factors, the algorithm cuts 2( y − x) = 2 vertices of color 

c3 from F 11 and merges them into F 12 , yielding the final fair clustering F2 = Fmpf .When merging these two sub-blocks into a single block Bti = Bt−12i−1 ∪ Bt−12i , our goal is to make the entire set of colors jointly proportional to their global ratios (pa1 , . . . , p as , p b1 , . . . , p bu ). This is possible only if both sub-blocks share the same scaling factor, i.e., x = y. If x̸ = y, then one group of colors is overrepresented relative to the other. To correct this imbalance, make-pdc-fair performs a controlled adjustment. Suppose x < y—that is, the sub-block Bt−12i currently represents the global proportions at a larger scale. The algorithm reduces its contribution by removing pbk · (y − x) points of each color zbk uniformly across the cluster. If x > y then the sub-block Bt−12i under represents the global proportions. The algorithm increases its contribution by adding pbk · (x − y) points of each color zbk to match the total mass. After this operation, both sub-blocks contribute exactly the same total scaling factor 

x′ = y′ = min( x, y ), ensuring that within the combined block Bti , we have 

za1 (F t) : . . . : zas (F t) : zb1 (F t) : . . . : zbu (F t) = pa1 : . . . : pas : pb1 : . . . : pbu .

(see Fig. 1.5) At each step, the algorithm make-pdc-fair incurs an approximation of 6, thus giving an overall approximation of 7log |ζ| = |ζ|2.81 .Since in this setting paj ≥ pbk for all j ∈ [s] and k ∈ [u], the high-level idea behind the 6-approximation at each step of the algorithm make-pdc-fair closely mirrors the reasoning behind the 3-approximation guarantee of the algorithm MakeClustersFair in the two-color case. Recall that MakeClustersFair is the algorithm that transforms a pdc into a fair clustering when the vertex set is divided into two color groups. The additional multiplicative factor of 2 in the approximation bound of make-pdc-fair arises to account for overcounting in the optimal solution, since in the multi-color setting each block may contain multiple colors, and interactions between these colors can lead to double counting of pairwise contributions. 27 High-Level Idea of fair-equi . The algorithm fair-equi takes as input a clustering C whose vertices are colored from χ = {c1, c 2, . . . , c d}, where the colors are distributed equally but |χ| is not a power of two. The key idea is to combine the structural fairness guarantees of fairpower-of-two 

with the proportional balancing capability of make-pdc-fair .The algorithm proceeds as follows. First, we partition the color set χ into log |χ| disjoint groups 

G1, G 2, . . . , G log |χ| such that the size of each group Gj is a power of two. This is done greedily according to the binary representation of |χ|: for each index j ∈ [log ⌈| χ|⌉ ], if the corresponding bit in the binary representation of |χ| is 1, a group of size 2j−1 is created. Next, fair-equi applies fairpower-of-two independently within each color group Gj to obtain an intermediate clustering I = {I1, I 2, . . . , I η} satisfying that for every Ii ∈ I and every pair of colors 

ca, c b ∈ Gℓ,

ca(Ii) = cb(Ii).

Thus, fairpower-of-two ensures equal representation within each power-of-two-sized color group. We then treat each group Gℓ as a single meta-color zℓ ∈ ζ and invoke the make-pdc-fair 

subroutine on the intermediate clustering I. The clustering I serves as a p-divisible clustering because for each cluster Ii ∈ I , the count |Gℓ(Ii)| is divisible by |Gℓ|, where Gℓ(Ii) denotes the subset of vertices of Ii that has a color from the group Gℓ. Applying make-pdc-fair produces a final fair clustering F = {F1, . . . , F ι} such that for every cluster Fj ∈ F ,

|G1(Fj )| : |G2(Fj )| : . . . : |Glog |χ|(Fj )| = |G1| : |G2| : . . . : |Glog |χ||.

Since fairpower-of-two already enforces equality within each group Gℓ, the proportional balancing across groups performed by make-pdc-fair ensures that in the final clustering F, every color 

cu, c v ∈ χ satisfies cu(Fj ) = cv(Fj ). Thus, fair-equi outputs a globally fair clustering where all colors are equally represented in every cluster. As established earlier, the algorithm fairpower-of-two achieves an approximation factor of 

O(|χ|1.6). In the algorithm fair-equi , the number of meta-colors generated is at most log |χ|,and consequently, the subroutine make-pdc-fair contributes an additional approximation factor of O(log 2.81 |χ|). By applying the triangle inequality to combine the errors introduced by the two stages, the overall approximation factor of fair-equi becomes O(|χ|1.6 log 2.81 |χ|).

Arbitrary Representation. When the vertex set V is divided into disjoint color groups of ar-bitrary sizes, we design an algorithm fair-general to construct a fair clustering that reflects the global color distribution. Let the vertices be colored from the set χ = {c1, c 2, . . . , c d}, and suppose their total counts satisfy the global ratio 

c1(V ) : c2(V ) : · · · : c|χ|(V ) = p1 : p2 : · · · : p|χ|.

To achieve fairness under these non-uniform proportions, we adopt a two-step approach analogous to the two-color case. In the first step, we construct a p-divisible clustering (pdc ) M = {M1, M 2, . . . , M t} using our algorithm create-pdc . Recall in a pdc M, for every cluster M ∈ M and every color cj ∈ χ, the number of points of color cj in M is divisible by pj .In the second step, we transform the p-divisible clustering M into a fair clustering F using 

make-pdc-fair such that for every cluster F ∈ F ,

c1(F ) : c2(F ) : · · · : c|χ|(F ) = p1 : p2 : · · · : p|χ|.

28 High level idea of create-pdc : To construct a pdc , we proceed iteratively over the colors in 

χ. For each color cj , we apply the algorithm AlgoforGeneral introduced in the two-color case. At iteration j, the algorithm AlgoforGeneral is applied to the current clustering to ensure that, within every cluster, the number of vertices of color cj becomes a multiple of pj . Thus, after processing all colors sequentially, in the resulting clustering for all colors cj the number of vertices of color cj is divisible by pj .Recall that in the two-color setting, the algorithm AlgoforGeneral takes as input a clustering whose vertices are divided into two color groups—say blue and red—such that the global ratio of blue to red points is p : 1 . The output of AlgoforGeneral is a refined clustering where, in every cluster, the number of blue vertices is a multiple of p. By applying this procedure iteratively to each color cj , we generalize the same principle to the multi-color setting, thereby obtaining a pdc .As discussed earlier, the algorithm AlgoforGeneral achieves an approximation factor of 3.5

in the two-color setting. When AlgoforGeneral is applied iteratively across all colors in χ, these approximation factors accumulate, resulting in an overall approximation factor of O(|χ|) for the algorithm create-pdc . It is worth noting that if one were to apply a direct analysis using the triangle inequality, a constant-factor approximation ν at each step would compound multiplicatively, yielding an overall factor of ν|χ|. To avoid this exponential blow-up, we adopt a refined analytical approach inspired by Chakraborty et al. [CCD +25], originally developed for the two-color setting where the blue-to-red ratio is an arbitrary p : q. This sophisticated analysis allows us to bound the cumulative approximation additively rather than multiplicatively, thereby ensuring an O(|χ|)

guarantee for create-pdc .

High-Level Idea of fair-general : Given an input clustering C, the algorithm fair-general 

proceeds in two stages. First, it applies create-pdc to construct a pdc clustering M, ensuring that within each cluster, the number of points of color cj is divisible by pj . Subsequently, it invokes 

make-pdc-fair on M to produce the final fair clustering F.The algorithm create-pdc achieves an approximation factor of O(|χ|), while make-pdc-fair 

contributes an additional factor of O(|χ|2.81 ). Combining these guarantees, fair-general attains an overall approximation factor of O(|χ|3.81 ).

# Fair Consensus Clustering in the Streaming Setting 

In fair consensus clustering, the input consists of a collection of clusterings I = {C 1, . . . , Cm} defined on a common vertex set. The objective is to compute a fair clustering F such that

Our goal is to compute such a clustering F using only O(n log m) space and O n2 log 4 m + t1(n) log 3 m time, where t1(n) denotes the running time of an unweighted fair correlation clustering algorithm. We work in the streaming model pairwise streaming model described in Section 1.1. We now describe our streaming algorithm, denoted by algo-fair-con-stream .29 High-Level Idea of algo-fair-con-stream : Recall that in the pairwise consensus streaming model , each stream element is a triple (p, j, b ) that encodes information about the j-th clustering 

Cj ∈ I. Prior to processing the stream, we uniformly sample 4g log m indices j1, . . . , j 4g log m from

A key technical challenge in our setting is evaluating the objective value of a candidate clustering 

F′ ∈ eF. In the streaming model, computing its distance to all input clusterings Ci ∈ I is infeasible, since only O(log m) clusterings can be stored at any time. To overcome this limitation, we leverage a result due to Indyk [Ind99; Ind01]. Specifically, if we uniformly sample a subset W ⊆ I of size 

Ω( ε−2 log m), for any 0 < ε < 1, then with probability at least 1 − 1/m , the following guarantee holds: mX

Here, Fbest ,W denotes the fair clustering in eF that minimizes the objective when evaluated only on the sampled set W, while Fbest ,I denotes the fair clustering in eF that minimizes the objective when evaluated over the full set I.We therefore independently sample an additional 64 ε−2 log m clusterings from I to form the set W, and use W to approximately evaluate the objective function for all candidates in eF. Since we store only O(log m) clusterings in total, and each clustering requires O(n) space, the overall space complexity of the algorithm is O(n log m). To analyze the approximation factor and the time complexity of the algorithm, we need to look at the construction of eF.

Construction of the Candidate Set eF: After sampling 4g log m input clusterings, for each sampled clustering Ck (where k ∈ [4 g log m]), we compute and store its α-close fair clustering FCk

in the candidate set eF. In addition, for every triple (Cx, Cy, Cz ) of sampled clusterings, we perform the following steps. (i) We construct an unweighted correlation clustering instance Gx,y,z from the three clusterings 

Cx, Cy, and Cz . For each unordered vertex pair (u, v ), the edge (u, v ) in Gx,y,z is assigned a positive label if u and v are placed in the same cluster in at least two of the three clusterings; otherwise, the edge is assigned a negative label. (ii) We compute an unweighted fair correlation clustering Tx,y,z on the graph Gx,y,z using a known algorithm for unweighted fair correlation clustering. (iii) We add the clustering Tx,y,z to the candidate set eF.We refer to the above procedure as the cluster fitting algorithm, denoted by ClusterFitting .We now provide intuition for why the ClusterFitting algorithm is effective. 30 Let F∗ 

> con

denote an optimal fair consensus clustering. For each input clustering Ci ∈ I, let Ii

It can be shown that if at least m/g input clusterings are close to the optimal solution F∗

> con

,that is, if there exists a set of at least m/g indices such that

for some parameter β > 1, then with high probability our sampling procedure selects one such clustering Ci. In this case, the corresponding α-close fair clustering FCi yields an (α + 1 .995) -approximation to the optimal fair consensus clustering. The more challenging case arises when fewer than m/g clusterings are close to F∗

> con

. In this regime, the ClusterFitting algorithm becomes crucial. Specifically, consider triples (Cx, Cy, Cz ) for which each of |Ix|, |Iy|, and |Iz | is large—exceeding (1 − β)AVG —but whose pairwise intersections are bounded. That is, for all distinct r, s ∈ { x, y, z },

|Ir ∩ Is| ≤ α AVG .

For such triples, the fair clustering Tx,y,z produced by the ClusterFitting algorithm is close to the optimal solution F∗

> con

, and consequently achieves an (α + 1 .995) -approximation. The intuition underlying why the clustering Tx,y,z is close to F∗ 

> con

is as follows. Consider a vertex pair (u, v ) ∈ Ir∩Is

for some distinct indices r, s ∈ { x, y, z }. Without loss of generality, assume that u and v are clustered together in Cr and Cs. Since (u, v ) ∈ Ir ∩ Is, it follows that u and v are separated in the optimal fair consensus clustering F∗

> con

.By construction of the correlation clustering instance Gx,y,z , the edge (u, v ) receives a positive label whenever u and v are clustered together in at least two of the three clusterings Cx, Cy, and 

Cz . Therefore, for the pair (u, v ) under consideration, the edge (u, v ) is labeled positive in Gx,y,z .Consequently, in the unweighted fair correlation clustering Tx,y,z computed from Gx,y,z , vertices u

and v are placed in the same cluster. When comparing Tx,y,z to the optimal clustering F∗

> con

, the clustering Tx,y,z incurs a cost for the pair (u, v ), since u and v are clustered together in Tx,y,z but separated in F∗

> con

. Thus, every pair belonging to the intersection Ir ∩ Is necessarily contributes to the disagreement between Tx,y,z and 

F∗

> con

. Therefore, if the total number of such pairs is bounded, the overall disagreement between 

Tx,y,z and F∗ 

> con

is small, implying that Tx,y,z is close to F∗

> con

.We show that, with high probability, our sampling procedure selects clusterings Cx, Cy, and Cz

whose corresponding pairwise intersections are bounded. Consequently, with high probability, the algorithm achieves an approximation factor of (α + 1 .995) .We now analyze the running time of the algorithm. The candidate set eF contains O(log 3 m)

clusterings. The dominating cost to construct these candidates is by applying the ClusterFitting 

procedure to all triples of sampled clusterings which requires O t1(n) log 3 m time. 31 To select the best clustering from eF, we evaluate the objective function for each candidate. This requires O(log 3 m) evaluations. In each evaluation, we compute the distance between the candidate clustering and each clustering in the sampled set W. Computing the distance between two clusterings takes O(n2) time, and since |W| = O(log m), the total time per evaluation is 

O(n2 log m). Therefore, the total time required for all evaluations is O(n2 log 4 m).Combining these bounds, the overall running time of the algorithm is 

O n2 log 4 m + t1(n) log 3 m .

# 1.4 Organization 

The remainder of this thesis is organized as follows. In Chapter 2, we present our results on fault-tolerant reachability preservers. The Chapter 3 studies the closest fair clustering problem when the vertex set V is partitioned into two disjoint color groups, while Chapter 4 extends these results to settings with more than two color groups. In Chapter 5, we explore the implications of our closest fair clustering framework for fair correlation clustering and fair consensus clustering. The Chapter 6 presents our algorithmic results for fair consensus clustering in the streaming setting. Finally, Chapter 7 outlines several open problems and directions for future research. 32 Chapter 2 

# Reachability Preservers 

In this chapter, we present a construction of a dual fault-tolerant reachability preserver (2-FTRS )for a given directed graph G = ( V, E ) and a vertex-pair set P ⊆ V × V . Specifically, we establish Theorem 1, which we restate below. 

Theorem 1 (Upper Bound on 2-FTRS ). For any directed graph G = ( V, E ) with n vertices and a set P ⊆ V × V of vertex-pairs, there exists a 2-FTRS (G, P) having at most O(n4/3|P| 1/3) edges. Furthermore, we can find such a subgraph in polynomial time. 

To prove this result, we first describe an alternate construction of a 2-FTRS for a single vertex pair. We then extend this construction to handle multiple pairs, thereby obtaining the general result. 

# 2.1 An (Alternate) Construction of 2-FTRS for a Single Pair 

Given any directed graph G with n vertices, for any pair of vertices (s, t ), we already get an O(n)

size 2-FTRS (G, (s, t )) from [BCR16]. Let us state the result of [BCR16] formally here. 

Theorem 12 ([BCR16]) . For any directed graph G = ( V, E ), a designated source vertex s ∈ V ,and an integer k ≥ 1, there exists a (sparse) subgraph H of G which is a k-FTRS (G, {s} × V ) and contains at most 2kn edges. Moreover, such a subgraph is computable in O(2 kmn ) time, where n

and m are respectively the number of vertices and edges in graph G.

However, now we study the structure of a 2-FTRS (G, (s, t )) more closely and provide a slightly different construction which will be useful for our construction of 2-FTRS for multiple pairs. Let us first define a notation to denote the ordering of vertices on a specific path. For any path P and two vertices u, v on P , we will write u < P v if and only if u appears before v on P .Suppose we are given a directed graph G and a pair (s, t ). Let us consider two s − t paths P 1(s,t )

and P 2(s,t ), that intersect only at s − t cut-edges and s − t cut-vertices. We call these two paths 

outer strands . Next, we define coupling paths between these two outer strands. For each vertex on the outer strands, let us first define two coupling points of it (one on each outer strand). We call an s − t path in H(s,t ) nice if it first follows a outer strand till some vertex u, then take a coupling path to some vertex u′, and finally follows the outer strand on which u′ lies till t. # 2.2 Dual Fault-tolerant Pairwise Reachability Preserver 

In this section, we provide the construction of 2-FTRS for multiple pairs, and thus prove Theorem 1. To prove Theorem 1 we provide a construction of 2-FTRS with slack . Let us define it below. 

Sparsifiers with Slack. In case of pairwise FTRS we want to construct an subgraph that provides correct reachability information for all the given vertex-pairs even after edge failures. This is the most standard definition of pairwise FTRS. Next, we define a variant which we refer to as pairwise FTRS with slack . Interested readers may find more works on sparsifiers with slack in [CDG06; KRX +07; Din07; Bod22]. Given a graph G and a pair set P, a subgraph H is a k-FTRS with slack if there is P′ ⊆ P , |P ′| = Ω( |P| ) such that H is a k-FTRS for the pair set P′.35 The following lemma from [Bod22] shows that to construct a k-FTRS for a pair set, it suffices to construct a k-FTRS with slack. 

Lemma 2.2.1 ( [Bod22]) . Let us consider constants α, β, γ > 0 and a parameter p∗. Consider a graph G(V, E ) with n vertices. If 

• There is a k-FTRS of size O(nα) for any vertex-pair set P of size at most p∗, and 

• There is a k-FTRS with slack of size O(nβ |P| γ ) for any vertex-pair set P of size at least p∗,then there is a k-FTRS (G, P) of size at most O(nα + nβ |P| γ ). Moreover, if the k-FTRS for at most p∗ pairs and the k-FTRS with slack for at least p∗ pairs can be computed in polynomial time, then the k-FTRS (G, P) can also be computed in polynomial time. 

Thus, due to Lemma 2.2.1 to prove Theorem 1 it suffices to construct 2-FTRS with slack. To construct that, we need the concept of fractional hitting set .

Fractional Hitting Set. Let U := {1, 2, · · · , n } be the universe. A set S ⊆ U is said to be a 

hitting set of a family of subsets S1, S 2, · · · , S m ⊆ U if and only if for all i ∈ [m], S ∩ Si̸ = ∅ (i.e., S

intersects all the subsets Si). Given a family of subsets, the problem of finding the minimum sized hitting set is referred to the hitting set problem , and is known to be NP-complete. However, there is a simple greedy algorithm that given any family of subsets S1, · · · , S m each of size at least k, finds a hitting set of size at most O( nk log n).In this thesis, we consider a variant of the hitting set problem, which we call fractional hitting set . A set S ⊆ U is said to be a hitting set with slack of a family of subsets S1, · · · , S m if and only if |{ i ∈ [m] | S ∩ Si̸ = ∅}| = Ω( m) (i.e., S has a non-empty intersection with at least a constant fraction of the input subsets). It is not difficult to see that the above mentioned greedy hitting set algorithm finds a fractional hitting set of size at most O(n/k ).

Theorem 13. There is a deterministic algorithm that given a family of subsets S1, · · · , S m ⊆ [n]

each of size at least k, finds a fractional hitting set of size at most O(n/k ) in time O(n+mk + nk log n).Proof. W.l.o.g. assume, each Si is of size exactly k; otherwise we can just consider any k elements from each Si. Let us now consider a slightly modified version of the standard greedy algorithm for the hitting set problem. Initialize F := {S1, · · · , S m}, and S = ∅. For each v ∈ [n], compute the number of subsets in F that contains v, i.e., c(v) := |{ Si ∈ F | v ∈ Si}| . Let vmax be a maximizer of c(v). Include vmax in S, i.e, S ← S ∪ { vmax }. Remove all the subsets that contains vmax from 

F, i.e., F ← F \ { Si | vmax ∈ Si}. Continue this process R = 4 n/k times, and output S. (Note, instead to continuing the process until F = ∅ as in the standard greedy algorithm for the hitting set problem, we terminate after a fixed number of steps.) Clearly, |S| = R = O(n/k ). We would like to claim that S is a fractional hitting set. For that purpose, it suffices to show that at the end of the algorithm |F| ≤ m/ 10 . Let nj denote the size of 

F after the j-th element is added in S. n0 = m, and we need to show that nR ≤ m/ 10 . Consider the step when the j-th element has been added in S. Observe, P

For the sake of completeness, let us briefly comment on the running time analysis. At the beginning we can compute the values of c(v)’s, and then store them using any standard heap data structure (e.g. [BLT12]). Then at each step, we extract the max value, and then we update the values of the others. This gives the total time to be O(n + mk + nk log n).

Theorem 14. For any directed graph G = ( V, E ) with n vertices and a set P ⊆ V × V of vertex-pairs, there exists a 2-FTRS with slack having at most O(n4/3|P| 1/3) edges. Furthermore, we can find such a subgraph in polynomial time.

# Closest Fair Clustering with Two Colors 

In this chapter, we present an algorithm to construct a fair clustering F which is 17 -close to an input clustering C, when the irreducible ratio of blue to red vertices is p : 1 for some integer p > 1.More specifically, we prove Theorem 4, which we restate below. 

Theorem 4. Let the vertex set V be partitioned into two disjoint groups, blue and red, where the ratio of blue to red vertices in V is p : 1 for some integer p > 1. Then there exists a polynomial-time algorithm that computes a 17 -close fair clustering with respect to a given input clustering C.

Our approach proceeds in two stages. In the first stage, given an input clustering C, we compute a p-divisible clustering T . In the second stage, we take T as input and produce a fair clustering F.

Definition 3.0.1 (p-Divisible Clustering) . Let V be a set of vertices colored from the set χ =

{c1, . . . , c r}, where the color proportions in V satisfy 

p1 : p2 : · · · : pr = c1(V ) : c2(V ) : · · · : cr(V )

and ci(V ) denotes the number of vertices of color ci in V . A clustering T = {T1, . . . , T m} of V is called a p-divisible clustering if, for every cluster Ti ∈ T and each color cj ∈ χ, the count cj (Ti) of vertices of color cj in Ti is divisible by pj ; that is, pj | cj (Ti).In the special case of two colors—blue and red—where the global ratio of blue to red vertices is 

p : 1 , a p-divisible clustering T ensures that, for every cluster Ti ∈ T , the number of blue vertices 

|blue (Ti) | is a multiple of p. We use the abbreviation pdc to denote a p-divisible clustering. A clustering T is said to be δ-close pdc to an arbitrary unfair clustering D if 

dist( D, T ) ≤ δ · dist( D, T ∗),

where T ∗ denotes the closest pdc to D.Finally, we complement our upper bound result by showing that the problem becomes NP-hard when the ratio between the number of blue and red vertices is p : q with p̸ = q.

# 3.1 Creating a p-divisible clustering. 

In this section, given a clustering C = {C1, . . . , C m} of a set of red-blue colored vertices, where the irreducible ratio of blue to red vertices is p for some integer p > 1, we construct a pdc T =

{T1, T 2, . . . , T ζ }. Our main result for this section is the following: 40 Lemma 3.1.1. There exists a polynomial time algorithm that, given any clustering C on n colored vertices with each vertex having a color either red or blue and a non-negative integer p, finds its 

3.5-close pdc .

We provide the algorithm to find a 3.5-close pdc in Section 3.1.1 and provide the analysis of the algorithm in Section 3.1.2 

3.1.1 Details of the Algorithm 

Before describing our algorithm, let us first introduce a few notations that we use later. For any input cluster Ci, if its number of blue vertices is not a multiple of p, then we define its surplus as the minimum number of blue vertices whose removal makes the number of blue vertices a multiple of p, and we define its deficit as the minimum number of blue vertices whose addition makes the number of blue vertices to be a multiple of p. Formally, we define them as follows. 

• Surplus of Ci, s(Ci): For any cluster Ci ⊆ V , s(Ci) = |blue (Ci) | mod p.

• Deficit of Ci, d(Ci): For any cluster Ci ⊆ V if p ∤ |blue (Ci) | then d(Ci) = p − s(Ci) otherwise 

d(Ci) = 0 .We call the operation of taking some vertices, S (say) from a cluster Ci, that is Ci = Ci \ S

as cut and the operation of merging some vertices M (say) to a cluster Ci that is Ci = Ci ∪ M as 

merge . Let us define some more terms that we need to describe our algorithms. 

• Cut cost, κ(Ci): For any cluster Ci ∈ C , κ(Ci) = ( s(Ci) · (|Ci| − s(Ci)) .

• Merge cost, μ(Ci): For any cluster Ci ∈ C , μ(Ci) = d(Ci) · | Ci|.

Description of the algorithm AlgoforGeneral . Given a clustering, C, we partition the clusters 

Ci ∈ C into two types based on the s(Ci) value of the cluster Ci. If s(Ci) ≤ p/ 2 we call these clusters as cut clusters and if s(Ci) > p/ 2 we call these clusters as merge clusters. Thus 

• cut = {Ci | s(Ci) ≤ p/ 2}.

• merge = {Ci | s(Ci) > p/ 2}.We first sort the clusters Ci ∈ merge based on the value of (κ(Ci) − μ(Ci)) in non-increasing 

order. Our algorithm AlgoforGeneral cuts a set of s(Ci) blue vertices from each cluster Ci ∈ cut 

and merges them to clusters in merge sequentially starting from the beginning in the sorted list. In each cluster Cj ∈ merge , we merge a set of d(Cj ) blue vertices. Now, we have two cases: after a certain vertex of time, the remaining set of clusters in cut becomes empty, and we are only left with some clusters in merge ; in this case, we call our subroutine AlgoforMerge . To remove any ambiguity, we refer to the remaining clusters in merge at this vertex as merge ′, and the set of clusters created from cut after cutting the surplus vertices s(Ci) from each cluster Ci ∈ cut be 

newcut .Another case is when the remaining set of clusters in merge becomes empty, and we are only left with some clusters in cut . In this case, we call our subroutine AlgoforCut . Again, To remove any ambiguity, we refer to the remaining set of clusters in cut as cut ′.41 Description of the Subroutine AlgoforMerge . The subroutine AlgoforMerge takes as input two sets of clusters: newcut and merge ′. Here, newcut denotes the set of clusters that have already become p-divisible after the initial phase of AlgoforGeneral , and merge ′ denotes the remaining clusters from merge whose number of blue vertices is not yet a multiple of p.We begin by computing the total deficit of the clusters in merge ′, defined as 

W = X 

> Cj∈merge ′

d(Cj ).

Intuitively, W represents the total number of blue vertices that must be added (through merging) across all clusters in merge ′ to make them p-divisible. For every cluster Ck ∈ newcut ∪ merge ′, we partition the set of blue vertices blue (Ck) into 

|blue (Ck) | − s(Ck)

p

subsets of size p, and one remaining subset of size s(Ck). We number the subset of size s(Ck) as the 0th subset , and label all other subsets of size p sequentially starting from 1. We denote the zth subset of cluster Ck by Wm,z .Each subset Wm,z is associated with a cutting cost defined as follows: 

κ0(Ck) = s(Ck) · (|Ck| − s(Ck)) , (cost for cutting the 0th subset) , (3.1) 

κz (Ck) = p · (|Ck| − (zp + s(Ck))) , (cost for cutting the zth subset, z ≥ 1).

The subroutine AlgoforMerge is invoked in the situation where only clusters in merge remain non– p-divisible. Ideally, we would prefer to perform merge operations for all clusters in merge ,since for these clusters we have s(Ci) > p/ 2, which implies μ(Ci) < κ (Ci). However, in order to make every cluster Ci ∈ merge satisfy the divisibility condition p | | blue (Ci) |, we must also perform cut operations on some clusters to supply the required blue vertices. Therefore, we choose to cut from clusters Ci that have the smallest difference (κ(Ci) − μ(Ci)) , ensuring that the incurred cutting cost is not disproportionately large relative to their merging benefit. After the main algorithm AlgoforGeneral terminates, all clusters in newcut are already p-divisible. Nevertheless, it may still be more cost-effective to cut subsets of size p from some of these clusters in newcut than from certain clusters in merge ′. This means that the algorithm may cut from the same cluster multiple times if it minimizes the total cost. At each iteration of AlgoforMerge , among all clusters Cℓ ∈ newcut ∪ merge ′, the algorithm selects a cluster Ck with the minimum cost associated with its least uncut subset Wm,v m , where vm

denotes the smallest index corresponding to a subset of size p not yet cut from Ck. The cost of the subset Wm,v m is defined as: 

cost (Wm,v m ) = 

(

κvm (Ck), if vm ≥ 1,κ0(Ck) − μ(Ck), if vm = 0 . (3.2) If multiple clusters have the same minimum cost, the algorithm breaks ties arbitrarily. Let Ck

be the selected cluster. The algorithm then cuts the subset Wm,v m from Ck. If Ck ∈ merge ′, it is subsequently removed from merge ′ and the subset Wm,v m is merged sequentially into the first available clusters in merge ′, which are maintained in non-increasing order of (κ(Ci) − μ(Ci)) .The process continues iteratively until the total deficit W becomes zero. We later prove that exactly W/p subsets need to be cut to eliminate the entire deficit, thereby ensuring that all clusters become p-divisible. 42 Description of the Subroutine AlgoforCut . The subroutine AlgoforCut takes as input the set of clusters cut ′, which represents the remaining clusters in cut after the main algorithm 

AlgoforGeneral terminates. For each cluster Ci ∈ cut ′, the algorithm removes s(Ci) blue ver-tices—corresponding to its surplus—and collects these vertices to form new auxiliary clusters, each of size p. This process is repeated sequentially until, in every cluster Ci ∈ cut ′, the number of blue vertices becomes a multiple of p. As a result, all clusters in the final output satisfy the p-divisibility property. We provide the pseudocode of the algorithms AlgoforGeneral , AlgoforCut , AlgoforMerge 

in Algorithm 4, Algorithm 6 and Algorithm 5 respectively. Let the output of the algorithm 

AlgoforGeneral be T .

Runtime analysis of AlgoforGeneral . The expensive step in the AlgoforGeneral is that in the first step of the algorithm, we sort the clusters in merge which would take 

O (|merge | log( |merge |)) = O(n log n) time. The algorithm uses a vertex v ∈ V at most twice, once while cutting the vertex v from its parent cluster Ci ∈ C (say) and another when merging the vertex v to some cluster Cj̸ = Ci. Hence, the cutting and merging process of the algorithm can take at most O(n) time (recall, |V | = n). In the subroutine AlgoforMerge , at each iteration, the algorithm needs to find the cluster that has the minimum cost attached to its least-numbered subset. The find-Min operation can be done in O(1) by implementing a priority queue with a Fibonacci heap. Hence, overall the time complexity of AlgoforGeneral is O(|merge | log( |merge |) + |V |) = O(n log n).

Proof of Lemma 3.1.3. Let T be the output of AlgoforGeneral in the cut case. Let 

cost 1(T ), cost 2(T ), cost 3(T ), cost 4(T ) be the four costs that are paid by our algorithm when in 71 AlgoforGeneral , we are left with the clusters present only in cut . Lemma 3.1.3 is essentially showing that 

cost 1(T ) + cost 2(T ) + cost 3(T ) + cost 4(T ) ≤ 2 OPT + 1 .5OPT .

We complete the proof by applying Claim 3.1.20, which gives 

cost 1(T ) + cost 2(T ) ≤ 2 OPT ,

and applying Claim 3.1.21, which gives 

cost 3(T ) + cost 4(T ) ≤ 1.5OPT .

Hence, from Claim 3.1.20 and Claim 3.1.21 we get that T ∗ pays at most 3.5 OPT in the cut case. 

# 3.2 Making a p-divisible clustering fair. 

Given a p-divisible clustering T = {T1, T 2, . . . , T ζ }, where the vertex set V is partitioned into two color groups, blue and red, with the ratio of blue to red vertices being p : 1 , our goal is to compute a closest fair clustering F∗ to T . Recall that a clustering is said to be fair if, in every cluster, the number of blue vertices is exactly p times the number of red vertices. Our main result in this section is stated below. 

Lemma 3.2.1. Let p > 1 be an integer, and let the vertex set V be partitioned into two color groups, blue and red, such that the ratio of blue to red vertices is p : 1 . Then, there exists a linear-time algorithm that, given any p-divisible clustering T , computes a 3-close fair clustering F to C.More specifically, 

dist( T , F) ≤ 3 · dist( T , F∗),

where F∗ denotes a closest fair clustering to T .

In Section 3.2.1, we introduce the algorithm MakeClustersFair (Algorithm 7), which takes T as its input, and outputs a fair cluster F. We analyze in Section 3.2.2 that dist( T , F) ≤ 3dist( T , F∗).

3.2.1 Details of the Algorithm 

The high-level idea of MakeClustersFair (Algorithm 7) is the following. The pdc T is parti-tioned into two sets. Each cluster Ti is chosen to be in one of the two sets based on whether 

|blue (Ti) | < p |red (Ti) | or |blue (Ti) | > p |red (Ti) |. If it is the former case, Ti is placed into the first set RedHeavy , while if it is the latter case, Ti is placed into the second set BlueHeavy . After that, a set of |red (Ti) | − |blue (Ti) |

p red vertices are cut from each cluster Ti ∈ RedHeavy . These vertices are merged to clusters in the set BlueHeavy . Each cluster Tj ∈ BlueHeavy are merged with exactly |blue (Tj |)

p − | red (Tj ) | red vertices. 72 Runtime Analysis of MakeClustersFair . First note, each vertex is used at most twice during the execution of the algorithm MakeClustersFair – once when we cut the red vertex v ∈ V from the cluster in the set RedHeavy and again when we merge that red vertex v ∈ V to cluster that belongs to the set BlueHeavy . Hence, the total runtime of the algorithm MakeClustersFair would be O(|V |).

3.2.2 Approximation Guarantee of MakeClustersFair 

In this section, we aim to prove Lemma 3.2.1. Consider a cluster Ti ∈ T . Suppose that in F∗, Ti is spread through clusters Xi, 1 ∪ Bi, 1 ∪

Ri, 1, X i, 2 ∪ Bi, 2 ∪ Ri, 2, . . . , X i,t ∪ Bi,t ∪ Ri,t , such that Ti = ∪tj=1 Xi,j , and Bi,j , R i,j are the set of blue vertices and red vertices from some clusters Tk (k̸ = i), respectively. Then the distance between the two clusterings F∗ and T is 

dist( T , F∗) = X

Lemma 3.2.2. Given positive numbers x1, x 2, . . . , x t and a positive integer S such that S ≤ Pti=1 xi.Then for any a1, a 2, . . . , a n such that ai ≥ 0, there exists non-negative integers y1, y 2, . . . , y t satisfy-ing: 

• 0 ≤ yi ≤ ⌈ xi⌉, for i = 1 , 2, . . . , t .

• Pti=1 yi = S.

• Pti=1 aiyi ≤ Pti=1 aixi.Proof. Without loss of generality, assume that a1 ≤ a2 ≤ · · · ≤ an.We will show that the output of the following algorithm is the desired yi’s. Let yi’s be the output of Algorithm 1. 

• 0 ≤ yi ≤ ⌈ xi⌉, for i = 1 , 2, . . . , t .73 Algorithm 1: Choose (y1, y 2, . . . , y t).

Observe that yi is decreased by 1 (Algorithm 1) only if yi > 0, and is increased by 1 (Algorithm 1) only if yi < ⌈xi⌉. Hence, each yi output by this algorithm satisfies 0 ≤ yi ≤ ⌈ xi⌉.

• Pti=1 yi = S.To see this, we consider (y1, y 2, . . . , y t) after the execution of each while loop. Initially, yi is set to ⌊xi⌋. If Pti=1 yi > S , the first while loop gradually decreases Pti=1 yi by 1 by decreasing a nonzero yi by 1, until Pti=1 yi = y. Since min Pti=1 yi = 0 and S ≥ 0, there must be an iteration where Pti=1 yi = S. After this iteration, the first while loop is done. The second while loop will not be executed, and the algorithm will return (y1, y 2, . . . , y t) satisfying Pti=1 yi = S.On the other hand, if Pti=1 ⌊xi⌋ < y , the first while loop is not executed. The second while loop gradually increases Pti=1 yi by 1 by increasing a yi < ⌈xi⌉ by 1, until Pti=1 yi. Since S ≤ Pti=1 xi ≤Pti=1 ⌈xi⌉, there always exists some yi(≤ ⌈ xi⌉) that can be increased whenever Pti=1 yt < S . Hence, there must be an iteration where Pti=1 yi = S. After this iteration, the second while loop is done, and the algorithm outputs (y1, y 2, . . . , y t) such that Pti=1 yi = S.

• Pti=1 aiyi ≤ Pti=1 aixi.We first claim that there exists m ≤ t such that for all i < m , we have yi ≥ xi, and for all i ≥ m,we have yi ≤ xi.If yi ≤ xi for all j, then m = 1 satisfies the claim. If there exists yi > x i, since Pti=1 yi < Pti=1 xi,there also exists yi < x i. Let m be the minimum index such that ym < x m, then yi ≥ xi for all 

j < m . Now we show that yi ≤ xi for all j ≥ m.To see this, note that in Algorithm 1, if the second while loop is not executed, then the algorithm only decreases yi. Thus yi ≤ ⌊ xi⌋ ≤ xi for all j. The second while loop is executed only if the first while loop is not. In that case, the second while loop starts with yi = ⌊xi⌋. If yi is increased (Algorithm 1), then yi = ⌈xi⌉ > x i. Assume to the contrary that there exists j > m such that 

yj > x j . This implies that before yj is increased, Pti=1 yi < S . This means that in the iteration of the for loop where i = m, we have Pti=1 yi < S and by definition of m, ym < x m. It follows that ym

is increased and becomes greater than xm, which is a contradiction since in the output, ym < x m.This concludes that yi ≤ xi, for all i ≥ m.Finally, we show that Pti=1 aiyi ≤ Pti=1 aixi. We have

RedHeavy would have been merged to some cluster in BlueHeavy .The distance dist( T , F) is the number of pairs of vertices that are clustered together in one clustering (either T or F) and are separated by the other (either F or T ). # 3.3 Concluding the Approximation Guarantee 

In this section, we prove Theorem 4 by combining the results of Lemma 3.2.1 and Lemma 3.1.1 to establish the overall approximation guarantee. Specifically, we show that the clustering F ob-tained by first applying AlgoforGeneral to the input clustering C to obtain T , and then applying 

MakeClustersFair to T , yields a 17 -close fair clustering with respect to C. Instead of directly proving Theorem 4, we first prove the following lemma, which immediately implies Theorem 4 by setting the parameters δ = 3 .5 and α = 3 .

Lemma 3.3.1. Consider any δ, α ≥ 1. Suppose there is a t1(n)-time algorithm Algorithm-A() that, given any clustering on n points, produces an δ-close pdc , and a t2(n)-time algorithm Algorithm-B() that, given any pdc on n points, produces a α-close fair clustering. Then there is an O(t1(n) + 

t2(n) + n)-time algorithm that, given any clustering on n points, produces an (δ + α + δα )-close fair clustering. Proof. Consider the following simple algorithm: Given a clustering C as input, first run Algorithm-A() to generate a pdc T , and then run Algorithm-B() with T as input to generate a fair clustering 

F. Finally, output F.It is straightforward to see that the running time of the above algorithm is O(t1(n) + t2(n) + n).We now claim that F is an (δ + α + δα )-close fair clustering of the input clustering C.80 Let F∗ be an (arbitrary) closest fair clustering of the input C, and T ∗ be an (arbitrary) closest 

pdc of the input C. Since by the definition, any fair clustering is also a pdc , we get 

dist( C, T ∗) ≤ dist( C, F∗). (3.57) Now, by the guarantee provided by Algorithm-A(), we get 

dist( C, T ) ≤ δ · dist( C, T ∗)

≤ δ · dist( C, F∗) (By Eq. (3.57)) . (3.58) Next, let C∗ be an (arbitrary) closest fair clustering of T (recall, T is an output of Algorithm-A()). Then, by the guarantee provided by Algorithm-B(), we get 

dist( T , F) ≤ α · dist( T , C∗)

≤ α · dist( T , F∗) (Since F∗ is a valid fair clustering) 

≤ α · (dist( T , C) + dist( C, F∗)) (By the triangle inequality) 

≤ α(δ + 1) · dist( C, F∗) (By Eq. (3.58)) . (3.59) Thus, finally, we deduce that 

dist( C, F) ≤ dist( C, T ) + dist( T , F) (By the triangle inequality) 

≤ δ · dist( C, F∗) + α(δ + 1) · dist( C, F∗) (By Eq. (3.58) and Eq. (3.59)) 

= ( δ + α + δα ) · dist( C, F∗)

and that concludes the proof. 

# 3.4 Hardness of Closest Fair Clustering Problem 

In this section, we prove that given a clustering C over a set of n red-blue colored vertices where the ratio between the total number of blue and red vertices is p for some integer p > 1, the problem of finding a Closest fair clustering F∗ is NP -hard. More specifically, we prove Theorem 5, which we restate below. 

Theorem 5. The closest fair clustering problem is NP -hard when the vertex set V is divided into two disjoint groups, blue and red, and the number of blue vertices is p times the number of red vertices, for some integer p > 1.

Let us start by defining the corresponding decision version of the problem. 

Definition 3.4.1 (closest p-fair ). Consider a positive integer p. Given a clustering C over a set of red-blue colored vertices where the ratio between the total number of blue and red vertices is p,and a non-negative integer τ , decide between the following: 

• YES: There exists a fair clustering (on input vertex set) F such that dist( C, F) ≤ τ ;

• NO: For every fair clustering (on input vertex set) F, dist( C, F) > τ .81 We show the following theorem. 

Lemma 3.4.2. For any integer p ≥ 2, the closest p-fair problem is NP -complete. 

It is immediate from Lemma 3.4.2 that Theorem 5 follows as a direct consequence. Hence, we focus on establishing Lemma 3.4.2. Observe that the closest p-fair problem clearly belongs to the class NP . To prove its NP -hardness, we present a polynomial-time reduction from the well-known 3-Partition problem (defined below) to closest p-fair . This reduction establishes that closest p-fair is NP -complete. 

Definition 3.4.3 (3-Partition problem) . Given a (multi)set of positive integers S = {x1, . . . , x n},decide whether (YES:) there exists a partition of S into m (disjoint) subsets S1, S 2, . . . , S m ⊆ S

where m = n/ 3, such that 

• For all i, |Si| = 3 ; and 

• For all i, P  

> xj∈Si

xj = T , where T =   

> P
> xj∈Sxj
> n/ 3

,or (NO:) no such partition exists. Note that, by partition of S we mean Si ∩ Sj = ∅ for all i̸ = j and Smi=1 Si = S.We know that the 3-Partition problem is in fact strongly NP -complete [GJ75], i.e., the 3-Partition problem remains NP -complete even if all the integers xi ∈ S are bounded by a polynomial in n;more specifically, max xi∈S xi ≤ nc for some non-negative constant c. Furthermore, the 3-Partition problem remains strongly NP -complete even when for all xi ∈ S, xi ∈ (T / 4, T / 2) , where T =

(T / 4, T / 2) ) variant of the 3-partition problem as the 3partition problem. 

Theorem 15. ([GJ75]) 3partition is strongly NP -complete. 

We divide our NP -hardness proof into two parts. First, we prove closest p-fair is NP -hard for any integer p ≥ 3, and then we prove closest p-fair is NP -hard also for p = 2 using a slightly different reduction. 

3.4.1 Hardness of closest p-fair for p ≥ 3:

In this section, we prove the following lemma. 

Lemma 3.4.4. For any integer p ≥ 3, the closest p-fair problem is NP -hard. 

To prove Lemma 3.4.4, we show a reduction from 3partition to closest p-fair .

Claim 3.4.9. Consider any integer p > 1. Suppose for a cluster Bi, there exists Y ki ⊆ F for some

such that dist( C, M) < dist( C, F∗) which contradicts the fact that F∗ is a closest fair clustering. 

Construction of M from F∗: Let Q ⊆ red (F ) such that |Q| = |Y ki |/p .

M = F∗ \ { F } ∪ { (Y ki ∪ Q), F \ (Y ki ∪ Q)}.

That is, in M we remove the cluster F from F∗ and add two clusters (Y ki ∪Q) and (F \(Y ki ∪Q)) .Since F is a fair cluster, both the clusters (Y ki ∪ Q) and F \ (Y ki ∪ Q) are fair. Let us now argue that dist( C, M) < dist( C, F∗). Roughly, the above construction only incurs an additional cost for pairs between Q and red (F ) \ Q, while saving a cost for pairs between Q and 

blue (F ) \ Y ki (note, blue (F ) \ Y ki = blue (F ) \ Bi). Further, note that |blue (F ) \ Bi| = p|red (F ) \ Q|.Thus, 

dist( C, M) ≤ dist( C, F∗) + |Q|| red (F ) \ Q| − p|Q|| red (F ) \ Q|

86 < dist( C, F∗) (for p > 1).

Claim 3.4.11. Consider any integer p > 1. If there exists a cluster Bi such that |Bi ∩ F | ≥ p for some F ∈ F ∗, then |blue (F ) \ Bi| ≤ p − (|Bi ∩ F | mod p).Proof. Let Yi = Bi ∩ F . M = F∗ \ { F } ∪ { (Yi ∪ W ∪ Q), F \ (Yi ∪ W ∪ Q)}

where W ⊆ blue (F )\Yi such that |W | = p−(|Yi| mod p) and Q ⊆ red (F ) such that |Q| = |Yi∪W |/p 

(note, |Yi ∪ W | is a multiple of p). That is, in M we remove the set F from F∗ and add two clusters (Yi ∪W ∪Q) and F \(Yi ∪W ∪Q).By the construction, M is a fair clustering. Observe that |Yi| = |Bi ∩ F | ≥ p > |W |. Then,

Proof of Lemma 3.4.4. By Lemma 3.4.5 and Lemma 3.4.6 we infer that for any p ≥ 3, S is a YES instance of the 3partition if and only if (C, τ ) is a YES instance of the closest p-fair . It now immediately follows from our reduction that for any p ≥ 3, closest p-fair is NP -hard because 

3partition is strongly NP -complete (Theorem 15). 

Remark 3.4.13 . We would like to remark that the above NP -hardness proof can be extended for any fixed p/q ratio on the input blue to red vertices as long as p/q > 1 + √2. More specifically, consider any two integers p, q ≥ 2 such that p/q > 1 + √2. Then the closest fair clustering problem on red-blue colored vertices where the ratio between the total number of blue and red vertices is 

p/q , is NP -hard. We can show this result by making the following modifications to the reduction: Take monochromatic red clusters Ri’s of size qx i, set τ = q2

pqT 2; everything else remain the same. The proof argument will be similar. One important vertex to note is that 

p/q > 1 + √2 is crucial for the argument used in the proof of Claim 3.4.7. Now in the following paragraph, we prove that closest p-fair is NP -hard also for p = 2 .

3.4.2 Hardness of closest p-fair for p = 2 :

In this section, we prove the following lemma. 

Lemma 3.4.14. For p = 2 , the closest p-fair problem is NP -hard. 

92 We again reduce the 3partition problem to the closest p-fair problem for the case when 

p = 2 . The reduction follows the same structure as in the case where p ≥ 3, with the only difference being the choice of the parameter τ . For completeness, we restate the reduction here.

Lemma 3.4.16. For p = 2 , if S is a NO instance of the 3partition , then (C, τ ) is also a NO instance of the closest p-fair .Proof. Let F∗ be any arbitrary closest clustering to C. We prove that dist( C, F∗) > τ .By the definition, dist( C, F∗) counts the total number of pairs (u, v ) that are together in C but separated by F∗ and the number of pairs (u, v ) that are in different clusters in C but together in 

F∗.To count a pair (u, v ), we use a charging scheme. For each pair (u, v ) we charge the vertex u,

1/2 and the vertex v, 1/2.Let us define cost (v) to be half times the number of vertices u such that u, v are together in C

but separated by F∗, or u, v that are in different clusters in C but together in F∗. More specifically, 

cost (v) := 12 |{ u |u, v ∈ C, C ∈ C but u ∈ Fa, v ∈ Fb, F a, F b ∈ F ∗, a ̸ = b

Claim 3.4.17. There exists ℓ, a 1, a 2 such that xa1,ℓ , x a2,ℓ ̸ = 0 .Proof. For the sake of contradiction, suppose for all ℓ, there exists at most one Fa ∈ F ∗ such that

# Closest Fair Clustering with more than Two Colors 

In this chapter, we find an approximate closest fair clustering to given clustering C, when the vertex set V is divided into multiple colored disjoint groups χ = {c1, . . . , c d}. We use the notation σ(v) to denote the color assigned to v from χ.

# 4.1 Approximate Closest Fair Clustering for Equi-Proportion Groups 

In this section, we provide an approximation algorithm to find a closest fair clustering when all the groups are of equal size. More specifically we prove Theorem 6, which we restate below. 

Theorem 6. Let χ be a set of colors and let V be partitioned into disjoint color classes indexed by χ, such that each color class has the same size and |χ| > 2, then there exists a polynomial-time algorithm that computes an O(|χ|1.6 log 2.81 |χ|)-close fair clustering with respect to a given input clustering C. Moreover, when |χ| is a power of two, the algorithm computes a O(|χ|1.6)-close fair clustering. 

First, let us handle the case when |χ| is a power of 2. To do that, we provide an algorithm 

fairpower-of-two , which produces an O(|χ|1.6)-close fair clustering Ffpt to a clustering C when |χ|

is a power of 2.

4.1.1 Details of the Algorithm: Power-of-Two Colors 

Let the input of the algorithm fairpower-of-two be a clustering C = {C1, C 2, . . . , C m}, where each cluster Ca ∈ C consists of points colored from a color set χ = {c1, c 2, . . . , c d}. We assume that the number of colors |χ| is a power of two. The goal is to produce a fair clustering Ffpt such that, in each cluster Fa ∈ F fpt , the number of points of each color is equal, that is cp(Fa) = cq(Fa) where p̸ = q and cr(Fa) denotes the number of points of color cr in Fa.The algorithm proceeds in log |χ| iterations, indexed by i = 1 to log |χ|. In each iteration, the color set χ = {c1, . . . , c |χ|} is partitioned into blocks of size 2i:

Bij = {c(j−1) ·2i+1 , . . . , c j·2i }, for j = 1 , . . . , |χ|/2i.

98 (Note: This defines |χ|/2i blocks in total.) Let, after iteration i of the algorithm fairpower-of-two the input clustering C is changed to a clustering N i. The algorithm fairpower-of-two at each iteration i maintains an invariant that for every block Bij and for every cluster N ia ∈ N i, the number of points of each color within Bij is equal in N ia. Formally, for all x, y ∈ { 1, . . . , 2i} with x̸ = y, we have: 

c(j−1) ·2i+x(N ia) = c(j−1) ·2i+y(N ia),

where cr(N ia) denotes the number of points of color cr in cluster N ia.Hence, after log |χ| iterations when there is only one block, we have that for the clustering 

N log |χ| = Ffpt in each cluster Fa ∈ F fpt , the number of points of each color is equal. Let us now describe the algorithm fairpower-of-two which maintains the above invariant. To describe the algorithm first we need to define some notations. 

• As described previously the clustering C changes at each iteration i of the algorithm 

fairpower-of-two .Let, N i = {N i

> 1

, N i

> 2

, . . . , N iz } be the clustering formed at iteration i.Specifically, the clustering C goes through the following transformations. 

C → N 1 → N 2 → · · · → N log |χ| = Ffpt .

• Bij (N ia): For any cluster N ia ∈ N i, we have Bij (N ia) denotes the set of points present in N ia

having color in the block Bij .More Specifically, 

Bij (N ia) = {v ∈ N ia | σ(v) ∈ Bij }

• Let T ja denote the surplus between Bij and Bij+1 within cluster N ia. If |Bij (N ia)| ≥ | Bij+1 (N ia)|,then the surplus is a subset of Bij (N ia) of size |Bij (N ia)| − | Bij+1 (N ia)| such that all colors 

cm, c ℓ ∈ Bij satisfy cm(T ja ) = cℓ(T ja ) for all cm̸ = cℓ. Otherwise, the surplus is defined analogously from Bij+1 (N ia).Now we are ready to describe the algorithm fairpower-of-two 

Description of fairpower-of-two : The algorithm proceeds in log |χ| iterative phases to enforce fairness across pairs of buckets. The high-level structure of the algorithm is as follows: 1. Initialization: Set the initial clustering N 0 = {N 01 , N 02 , . . . , N 0 

> f

} = C (input of 

fairpower-of-two ). 2. Iterative Refinement: For each phase i = 1 to log |χ|:(a) Initialize N i as a copy of N i−1.(b) Iterate over j = 1 to |χ|/2i in steps of 2i:i. For each cluster N ia ∈ N i:99 • Compute the surplus between the bucketed subsets Bij (N ia) and Bij+1 (N ia)i, de-fined as T ja .

• Remove this surplus from N ia.

• Store the removed surplus in set Sj if |Bij (N ia)| ≥ | Bj+1 (N ia)i|, else store it in 

Sj+1 .ii. Merge the stored surpluses using the subroutine multi-GM (Sj , S j+1 ) to form new fair clusters. iii. Add the resulting clusters of the multi-GM step back to N i.3. Output: Return the final fair clustering N log |χ|.

Remarks. The algorithm ensures local fairness between adjacent buckets Bij and Bij+1 by removing and redistributing surplus elements. As the phases progress, the granularity of this bal-ancing increases, resulting in a globally fair clustering. The multi-GM subroutine plays a key role in recombining the surplus elements in a way that preserves fairness across buckets. Let us now describe the multi-gm algorithm. 

Cescription of multi-gm : multi-gm is a procedure that takes as input two collections of point sets: 

• Set1 : a set of point subsets whose elements have colors from a color block Bij ,

• Set2 : a set of point subsets whose elements have colors from the adjacent color block Bij+1 .Each block Bij and Bij+1 contains the same number of colors. The goal is to iteratively pair subsets from Set1 and Set2 to form new subsets that are locally fair, meaning each resulting subset has an equal number of points from each color in the union of Bij ∪ Bij+1 . These fair subsets are returned as a new collection. The algorithm proceeds greedily by: 

• Iteratively selecting one subset from each of Set1 and Set2 .

• Comparing their sizes and extracting a balanced subset from the larger set to match the size of the smaller one. 

• Merging the two into a fair subset and adding it to the output collection. 

• Updating or removing the subsets used and repeating this process until no more valid pairings are possible. The final output is a collection of fair subsets, each containing an equal number of points of every color from Bij ∪ Bij+1 . We provide the pseudocode of the algorithms fairpower-of-two and 

multi-gm in Algorithm 8 and Algorithm 9 respectively. 100 4.1.2 Approximation Guarantee of fairpower-of-two 

In this section, we analyze the algorithm fairpower-of-two by establishing Lemma 4.1.1 stated below, 

Lemma 4.1.1. Given a clustering C as input, the algorithm fairpower-of-two computes a 

O(|χ|1.6)-close fair clustering, where |χ| is a power of 2.Proof. To prove this lemma, we need to define the following term 

• ith fairness constraint of algorithm fairpower-of-two : A clustering D is said to satisfy the 

ith fairness constraint of algorithm fairpower-of-two if, for every cluster D ∈ D and for all pairs of distinct colors ck̸ = cℓ in the set Bij , the number of points of color ck in D equals the number of points of color cℓ in D; that is, 

ck(D) = cℓ(D).

Now we prove the following claim 

Claim 4.1.2. In the algorithm fairpower-of-two , the clustering N i is 2-close clustering to N i−1

that satisfies the ith fairness constraint of fairpower-of-two , where N 0 = C.

We will prove Claim 4.1.2 later. First, let us prove Lemma 4.1.1 assuming Claim 4.1.2. To prove Lemma 4.1.1, we need to prove N log |χ|, the output of the algorithm fairpower-of-two 

is O(|χ|1.6)-close to C. Let, N ∗ be the closest fair clustering to C.We will prove using mathematical induction that after any iteration i of the algorithm 

fairpower-of-two we have 

dist( C, N i) ≤ (3 i − 1)dist( C, N ∗) (4.1) For the base case, that is when i = 1 by Claim 4.1.2 we have N 1 is a 2-close clustering to C that satisfies the 1st fairness constraint of algorithm fairpower-of-two . Since N ∗ also satisfies the 1st fairness constraint we have 

dist( C, N 1) ≤ 2dist( C, N ∗)

Now, let us assume Eq. (4.1) is true for i = k − 1, that is 

dist( C, N k−1) ≤ (3 k−1 − 1)dist( C, N ∗)

Now, we prove it for i = k.

dist( N k−1, N k) ≤ 2dist( N k−1, N ∗) (4.2) 

≤ 2(dist( N k−1, C) + dist( C, N ∗)) 

(triangle inequality) 

≤ 2((3 k−1 − 1)dist( C, N ∗) + dist( C, N ∗)) 

(by IH) 

= 2 · 3k−1dist( C, N ∗) (4.3) 101 Here, Eq. (4.2) is true because N ∗ also satisfies the ith fairness constraint of the algorithm 

fairpower-of-two and due to Claim 4.1.2. Now, we have 

dist( C, N k) ≤ dist( C, N k−1) + dist( N k−1, N k)

(triangle inequality) 

≤ (3 k−1 − 1)dist( C, N ∗) + 2 · 3k−1dist( C, N ∗)

(by IH and Eq. (4.3)) 

= (3 k − 1)dist( C, N ∗) (4.4) Hence, now we can conclude for i = log |χ|,

dist( C, N log |χ|) ≤ (3 log |χ| − 1)dist( C, N ∗)= O(|χ|1.6)dist( C, N ∗) (log 2 3 = 1 .6) (4.5) Thus the algorithm fairpower-of-two computes a O(χ1.6)-close fair clustering which completes the proof of Lemma 4.1.1. Now, let us prove Claim 4.1.2. To prove Claim 4.1.2, we establish two intermediate claims: Claim 4.1.3 and Claim 4.1.6. Let N i∗

denote the closest clustering to N i−1 that satisfies the ith fairness constraint of algorithm 

fairpower-of-two .In Claim 4.1.3, we derive a lower bound on the distance dist( N i−1, N i∗

), and in Claim 4.1.6, we provide an upper bound on the distance dist( N i−1, N i), where N i is the clustering obtained after the ith iteration of algorithm fairpower-of-two , starting from the initial clustering C.By comparing the bounds from Claim 4.1.3 and Claim 4.1.6, we will complete the proof of Claim 4.1.2. To formalize Claim 4.1.3, lets recall and introduce some notations:

It is straightforward to see that Claim 4.1.3 and Claim 4.1.6 proves Claim 4.1.2. Lemma 4.1.1 proves Theorem 6 when |χ| is a power of 2. Now, we prove Theorem 6 for any values of |χ|. To do that, we need to describe the algorithm fair-equi .108 4.1.3 Details of Algorithm: General Case 

To describe the algorithm fair-equi we need to use a subroutine make-pdc-fair . In the subroutine 

make-pdc-fair , we are given a clustering I = {I1, I 2, . . . , I η} over a point set V . Each point v ∈ V

is assigned a color from a finite set ζ = {z1, z 2, . . . , z r}. The total number of points of each color satisfies the global proportion: 

z1(V ) : z2(V ) : . . . : zr(V ) = p1 : p2 : . . . : pr

WLOG assume p1 > p 2 > · · · > p r.Furthermore, for each cluster Ii ∈ I , the number of points of each color zj is a multiple of pj .Recall from Chapter 3 such a clustering I is called p-divisible clustering (pdc for short). The goal of make-pdc-fair is to construct a clustering Fmpf such that for each cluster F ∈ F mpf ,

z1(F ) : z2(F ) : . . . : zr(F ) = p1 : p2 : . . . : pr

Description of the subroutine make-pdc-fair : The algorithm proceeds in ⌈log 2 r⌉ iterations. At each iteration, we refine the current clustering to more closely satisfy the target color proportions. Let F0 := I denote the initial clustering. The algorithm maintains a sequence of progressively refined clusterings: 

I = F0 → F 1 → F 2 → . . . → F ⌈log 2 r⌉ = Fmpf 

In each iteration t, the algorithm enforces proportionality over small blocks of colors, where the block size increases with t. As the iterations progress, these local balancing constraints compound, eventually ensuring that the final clustering F satisfies the full global ratio p1 : p2 : · · · : pr within every cluster. 

Description of the color blocks: We define a hierarchical merging of the color set ζ =

{z1, z 2, . . . , z r} over ⌈log 2 r⌉ iterations. At each iteration t, we maintain a partition of ζ into blocks 

{Bt

> 1

, B t

> 2

, . . . , B tmt }, where each block represents a group of colors whose proportions will be jointly enforced in that round. 

• Iteration 0: Initialize each color into its own singleton block: 

B0 

> j

= {zj } for j = 1 , 2, . . . , r 

• Iteration t ≥ 1: Merge adjacent blocks from the previous iteration: 

Bti = Bt−12i−1 ∪ Bt−12i for i = 1 , 2, . . . , 

j mt−1

2

k

If mt−1 is odd, then let Bt 

> ⌈mt−1/2⌉

= Bt−1 

> mt−1

(unchanged) 

• Continue this process until the final iteration T = ⌈log 2 r⌉, where a single block B⌈log 2 r⌉ 

> 1

= ζ

is formed. At each level t, we enforce the proportionality constraints jointly over the colors in each block 

Bti for all clusters in the current clustering Ft−1. This recursive construction guarantees that after 

⌈log 2 r⌉ rounds, the final clustering F⌈log 2 r⌉ satisfies the global target ratio p1 : p2 : · · · : pr in every cluster. 109 Balancing Rule: Consider a block Bti at iteration t, defined as: 

Bti = Bt−12i−1 ∪ Bt−12i

Let 

Bt−12i−1 = {za1 , z a2 , . . . , z as }, Bt−12i = {zb1 , z b2 , . . . , z bu }

where s ≥ u.By construction, at iteration t − 1, each cluster F t−1 ∈ F t−1 satisfies: 

za1 (F t−1) : . . . : zas (F t−1) = pa1 : . . . : pas

and 

zb1 (F t−1) : . . . : zbu (F t−1) = pb1 : . . . : pbu

Suppose the point counts in F t−1 satisfy: 

zaj (F t−1) = paj · x for j ∈ [s]

and 

zbk (F t−1) = pbk · y

for k ∈ [u].Our goal is to construct a cluster F t ∈ F t such that: 

za1 (F t) : . . . : zas (F t) : zb1 (F t) : . . . : zbu (F t)= pa1 : . . . : pas : pb1 : . . . : pbu

To achieve this, 

• If x > y , merge pbk · (x − y) points of color zbk into F t−1 for each k ∈ [u].

• If x < y , cut pbk · (y − x) points of color zbk from F t−1 for each k ∈ [u].This ensures that the scaling factor for both sub-blocks becomes equal ( x in both the cases), aligning the entire block Bti to the correct global ratio structure. We provide the pseudocode of 

make-pdc-fair in Algorithm 10. Let us now describe the algorithm fair-equi . The algorithm fair-equi takes a clustering C

as input. Each vertex of the clustering is colored from the colors χ = {c1, c 2, . . . , c d} where |χ| is not a power of two. 

Description of the algorithm fair-equi : We divide χ into log |χ| many disjoint color groups 

G1, G 2, . . . , G log |χ| such that the size of each group, |Gj | where j ∈ [log |χ|] is a power of two. We do it greedily according to the binary representation of |χ|. Consider the binary representation of 

|χ|. For each index j ∈ [log ⌈| χ|⌉ ], if the corresponding bit is 1, create a group of size 2j−1.We apply the algorithm fairpower-of-two to get an intermediate clustering I = {I1, . . . , I η}

such that for each cluster Ij ∈ I where j ∈ [k] we have for any pair of colors ca, c b ∈ Gℓ where 

ℓ ∈ [log |χ|],110 ca(Ij ) = cb(Ij )

For a subset S ⊆ V , let us define Gℓ(S) as the set of vertices v ∈ S such that v has a color in 

Gℓ. Now, our goal is to get a clustering F = {F1, . . . , F ι} from C such that for each cluster Fk ∈ F 

we have cu(Fk) = cv(Fk) where cu, c v ∈ χ.Given the intermediate clustering I as input, the algorithm make-pdc-fair produces a final fair clustering F = {F1, . . . , F ι} and thus for each cluster Fj ∈ F , the following proportion holds. 

|G1(Fj )| : |G2(Fj )| : . . . : |Glog |χ|(Fj )|

= |G1| : |G2| : . . . : |Glog |χ||

To apply make-pdc-fair , we consider a group of colors Gℓ as a single metacolor zℓ. Here the clustering I is a p-divisible clustering because for any cluster Ij ∈ I we have |Gℓ(Ij )| is divisible by 

|Gℓ|.Furthermore, the algorithm make-pdc-fair ensures uniformity within each group: for every group Gℓ and for any pair of colors ca, c b ∈ Gℓ with ℓ ∈ [log |χ|], it guarantees that ca(Fj ) = cb(Fj ).As a consequence, for any pair of colors cu, c v ∈ χ, we have cu(Fj ) = cv(Fj ), i.e., each color is equally represented within every cluster of F.

4.1.4 Approximation Guarantee of fair-equi 

To analyse fair-equi , we first analyze the subroutine make-pdc-fair for which we prove the following lemma. 

Lemma 4.1.7. The algorithm make-pdc-fair outputs a clustering F that is O(r2.81 )-close fair clustering to the input clustering I, where r is the number of colors. Proof. To prove this lemma, let us define the tth fairness constraint of make-pdc-fair algorithm. 

• tth fairness constraint of make-pdc-fair : Let C be a clustering and let {Btk} denote the color blocks at the tth iteration in the make-pdc-fair algorithm. We say that C satisfies the tth Fairness Constraint of make-pdc-fair if, for every cluster Ci ∈ C and every block Btk, the color counts satisfy: 

za1 (Ci) : za2 (Ci) : · · · : zam (Ci) = pa1 : pa2 : · · · : pam

where Btk = {za1 , . . . , z am }

To prove Lemma 4.1.7, we need to prove the following claim 

Claim 4.1.8. For all iterations t of the algorithm make-pdc-fair , Ft is a 6-close clustering to 

Ft−1 that satisfies the tth fairness constraint of make-pdc-fair . More specifically, 

dist( Ft−1, Ft) ≤ 6dist( Ft−1, Ft∗

)

where Ft∗

is the closest clustering to Ft−1 that satisfies tth fairness constraint of make-pdc-fair .

For now, let us assume Claim 4.1.8 and prove Lemma 4.1.7. 111 Proof of Lemma 4.1.7: To prove Lemma 4.1.7, we need to prove Flog r, the output of the algorithm make-pdc-fair is r2.8-close to I. Let F∗ be the closest fair clustering to I.We will prove using mathematical induction that after any iteration t of the algorithm 

make-pdc-fair we have 

dist( I, Ft) ≤ (7 t − 1)dist( I, F∗) (4.15) For the base case, that is when t = 1 by Claim 4.1.8 we have F1 is a 6-close clustering to I

that satisfies the 1st fairness constraint of algorithm make-pdc-fair . Since F∗ also satisfies the 1st fairness constraint we have 

dist( D, F1) ≤ 6dist( D, F∗)

Now, let us assume Eq. (4.15) is true for t = k − 1, that is 

dist( I, Fk−1) ≤ (7 k−1 − 1)dist( I, F∗)

Now, we prove it for t = k.

dist( Fk−1, Fk) ≤ 6dist( Fk−1, F∗) (4.16) 

≤ 6(dist( Fk−1, I) + dist( I, F∗)) (by triangle inequality )

≤ 6 · (7 k−1 − 1)dist( I, F∗) + 6dist( I, F∗)(by inductive hypothesis )= 6 · 7k−1dist( I, F∗) (4.17) Here, Eq. (4.16) is true because F∗ also satisfies the kth fairness constraint of the algorithm 

make-pdc-fair and due to Claim 4.1.8. Now, we have 

dist( I, Fk) ≤ dist( I, Fk−1) + dist( Fk−1, Fk)(by triangle inequality )

≤ (7 k−1 − 1)dist( I, F∗) + 6 · 7k−1dist( I, F∗)(by inductive hypothesis and Eq. (4.17) )= (7 k − 1)dist( I, F∗) (4.18) Hence, now we can conclude for t = log r,

dist( I, Flog r) ≤ O(7 log r)dist( I, F∗)= O(r2.8)dist( I, F∗) ( as log 2 7 = 2 .8) (4.19) Thus the algorithm make-pdc-fair computes a O(r2.8)-close fair clustering which completes the proof of Lemma 4.1.7. Now, let us prove Claim 4.1.8. To prove Claim 4.1.8, we establish four intermediate claims: Claim 4.1.9, Claim 4.1.10, Claim 4.1.11 and Claim 4.1.12. 112 Let Ft∗

denote the closest clustering to Ft−1 that satisfies the tth fairness constraint of algorithm 

make-pdc-fair .In Claim 4.1.9, Claim 4.1.10 and Claim 4.1.11 we derive lower bounds on the distance 

dist( Ft−1, Ft∗

), and in Claim 4.1.12, we provide an upper bound on the distance dist( Ft−1, Ft),where Ft is the clustering obtained after the tth iteration of algorithm make-pdc-fair , starting from the initial clustering I.By comparing the bounds from Claim 4.1.9, Claim 4.1.10, Claim 4.1.11 and Claim 4.1.12, we will complete the proof of Claim 4.1.8. Proof of Theorem 6. To prove this theorem, we analyse the algorithm fair-equi . The algorithm 

fair-equi takes a clustering C as input, applies the algorithm fairpower-of-two as a subroutine to create an intermediate clustering I and then applies the subroutine make-pdc-fair to get the final fair clustering F.By Lemma 4.1.7 we get that, F is O(log 2.8 |χ|)-close to the clustering I. By Lemma 4.1.1 we get that I is O(|χ|1.6)-close to the clustering C. By applying the triangle inequality to the two preceding results, we conclude that the output clustering F produced by fair-equi is O(|χ|1.6 log 2.8 |χ|)-close to the input clustering C.Let F∗ be the closest fair clustering to C. Hence, we get, 

dist( C, F) ≤ dist( C, I) + dist( I, F) (triangle inequality) 

≤ dist( C, I) + O(log 2.8 |χ|)dist( I, F∗)

≤ O(|χ|1.6)dist( C, F∗) + O(log 2.8 |χ|)dist( I, F∗)

≤ O(|χ|1.6)dist( C, F∗) + O(log 2.8 |χ|)(dist( C, I)+ dist( C, F∗)) (triangle inequality) 

≤ O(|χ|1.6 log 2.8 |χ| + |χ| + log 2.8 |χ|)dist( C, F∗)

≤ O(|χ|1.6 log 2.8 |χ|)dist( C, F∗)

This completes the proof of Theorem 6. 

# 4.2 Approximate Closest Fair Clustering for Arbitrary-Proportion Groups 

In this section, we extend our analysis from the equiproportion setting to the general case where color groups may appear in arbitrary proportions. In this setting, we provide a polynomial-time algorithm to compute an approximate closest fair clustering to a given input clustering C. More specifically, we prove Theorem 7, which we restate below. 121 Theorem 7. There is a polynomial time algorithm that, given an arbitrary clustering C over a vertex set V where each vertex v ∈ V has a color in χ = {c1, . . . , c k}, finds an O(|χ|3.81 )-close fair clustering to the clustering C.

To prove the above theorem, we take a 2-step approach similar to Chapter 3, which was con-strained to only two colors. (i) First, we convert the clustering C to a clustering M such that for each cluster Mi ∈ M , cj (Mi)

is divisible by pj . Recall, we call such a clustering a p-divisible clustering. (ii) In the second step, we will provide the clustering M as input to the algorithm make-pdc-fair 

and get a fair clustering F as output. Now, let us describe the fair-general that computes an approximate closest fair clustering to a given input clustering C. We first describe a subroutine create-pdc that converts a clustering C

on a vertex set V to a p-divisible clustering M.

4.2.1 Details of the Algorithm 

To describe the algorithm create-pdc , let us define some terms. 

• σ(Ck, c j ): Surplus of Ck w.r.t. to the color cj , formally, 

σ(Ck, c j ) ⊆ Ck

that contains cj (Ck) mod pj points of color cj if pj ∤ cj (Ck), otherwise it contains pj points of color cj .

• σj = P  

> Ck∈C

σ(Ck, c j ) (Note σj must be a multiple of pj )

• δ(Ck, c j ): Ceficit of Ck w.r.t. to the color cj , δ(Ck, c j ) ⊆ V \ Ck that contains pj − | σ(Ck, c j )|

points. 

• κj (Ck): Cut cost of Ck w.r.t. a color cj , formally 

κj (Ck) = |σ(Ck, c j )|(|Ck| − | σ(Ck, c j )|)

• μj (Ck): Merge cost of Ck w.r.t. a color cj , formally 

μj (Ck) = |δ(Ck, c j )|(|Ck|)

Now, let’s discuss the algorithm create-pdc .

Input: A clustering C of data points, a set of colors χ = {c1, c 2, . . . , c d}, and a desired proportion vector p = ( p1, . . . , p d) such that c1(V ) : c2(V ) : · · · : cd(V ) = p1 : p2 : · · · : pd.

Goal: Convert the input clustering C into a p-divisible clustering M where each cluster contains a multiple of pj points for each color cj .

Description of the subroutine create-pdc :

• For each color cj , prepare σj /p j empty extra clusters {P1, P 2, . . . , P σj /p j }

122 • For each cluster Ci ∈ C :

– If the number of surplus cj -colored points in Ci is small ( ≤ pj /2), that is |σ(Ci, c j )| ≤ pj /2

mark Ci for cutting ( cut set). 

– Otherwise, mark it for merging ( merge set). 

• While there are clusters in the cut set: 

– cut the surplus σ(Ck, c j ) from cluster Ck, that is Ck = Ck \σ(Ck, c j ) and try to distribute it: 

∗ If there are clusters in Cℓ ∈ merge , donate surplus to them using their deficits. That is 

Cℓ = Cℓ ∪ T. 

where 

T ⊆ σ(Ck, c j )

of size min( |σ(Ck, c j )|, |δ(Cℓ, c j )|).

∗ Otherwise, distribute the surplus to a prepared cluster Pm ∈extra clusters , en-suring that each such cluster is of size pj . That is 

Pm = Pm ∪ Q. 

where 

Q ⊆ σ(Ck, c j )

of size min( pj , |σ(Ck, c j )|, |σ(Ck, c j )| − | Pm|).

• After all CUT clusters are processed, handle the remaining MERGE clusters: 

– Pick a cluster with the minimum cut-merge cost difference and distribute its surplus, similar to the above step. 

– Do it until all the deficits are satisfied. Note that in the previous step, we may pick a cluster multiple times. We provide the pseudocode of the algorithm create-pdc in Algorithm 11. Now, we are ready to describe the algorithm fair-general .

Description of the algorithm fair-general : The algorithm fair-general proceeds in two stages. First, we apply the algorithm create-pdc to the input clustering C to obtain a p-divisible clustering M. Then, we apply the algorithm make-pdc-fair on M to obtain the final fair clustering 

F.123 4.2.2 Approximation Guarantee of fair-general 

In this section, we analyze the algorithm fair-general presented earlier and show that it satisfies the approximation guarantee stated in Theorem 7. To analyze fair-general , first let us analyze the subroutine create-pdc . More specifically, we prove the following lemma.

Proof of Theorem 7. To prove the theorem, we describe an algorithm fair-general that proceeds in two stages. First, we apply the algorithm create-pdc to the input clustering C to obtain a 

p-divisible clustering M that is O(|χ|)-close to C (by Lemma 4.2.1). Then, we apply the algorithm 

make-pdc-fair on M to obtain the final fair clustering F, which is O(|χ|2.81 )-close to M (by Lemma 4.1.7). By combining the guarantees from both steps, we conclude that F is O(|χ|3.81 )-close fair clustering to the input C.

dist( C, F) ≤ dist( C, M) + dist( M, F) (triangle inequality) 

≤ O(|χ|)dist( C, F∗) + O(|χ|2.81 )dist( M, F∗)

≤ O(|χ|)dist( C, F∗) + O(|χ|2.81 )(dist( C, M)+ dist( C, F∗)) (triangle inequality) 

≤ O(|χ| + |χ|3.81 + |χ|2.81 )dist( C, F∗)

≤ O(|χ|3.81 )dist( C, F∗)

This completes the proof of Theorem 7. 127 Chapter 5 

# Implications to Weighted Fair Correlation and Fair Consensus Clustering 

Building on the algorithms developed in the previous sections that compute an α-close fair clustering for a given (possibly unfair) clustering D, we now show how these results can be leveraged to obtain approximation algorithms for the weighted fair correlation clustering and fair consensus clustering problems.
