# Fair Consensus Clustering in Streaming setting 

In this chapter, we present an algorithm algo-fair-con-stream for solving fair consensus clustering in a streaming setting. Recall in the fair consensus clustering problem, the input is the set of clusterings I = {C1, . . . , C m} on a set of vertices V (with |V | = n) and our goal is to output a fair clustering F, such that 

> m

X

> i=1

dist( Ci, F)ℓ

!1/ℓ 

≤ β

> m

X

> i=1

dist( Ci, F∗

> con

)ℓ

!1/ℓ 

where F∗ 

> con

is an optimal fair consensus clustering, the distance, dist( ·, ·) between two clusterings is the measured by the number of pairs (u, v ) that are together in one clustering but separated by the other, and β ∈ R>1, ℓ ∈ Z>1 are some parameters. Let us now describe the streaming model. We consider an insertion-only streaming model. The input is a sequence of triples (p, j, b ), where: 

• p = ( u, v ) is an unordered pair of vertices in V ,

• j ∈ [m] identifies a clustering Cj ∈ I, and 

• b ∈ { 0, 1} indicates whether u and v lie in the same cluster in the clustering Cj (b = 0 ) or in different clusters ( b = 1 ). Here, a triple (( u, v ), j, b ) refers an information about the clustering Cj . For each clustering Cj ,we have n2 such tuples, hence the length of the stream is mn 2. These triples (p, j, b ) can arrive in any arbitrary order. Let us name the above streaming model as pairwise streaming model . Our main result is stated in the following theorem. 

Theorem 11. Suppose there exists an approximation algorithm for fair correlation clustering with running time t1(n), and assume oracle access to an α-approximate closest fair clustering algo-rithm. Then, there exists a (α + 1 .995) -approximation algorithm for fair consensus clustering in the 

pairwise streaming model that uses O(n log m) space and has query time 

O n2 log 4 m + t1(n) log 3 m .

133 6.1 Details of the algorithm: algo-fair-con-stream 

In this section, we present our streaming algorithm, algo-fair-con-stream for fair consensus clustering . The algorithm relies on a subroutine, denoted by find-candidates , which we describe first. The subroutine find-candidates takes as input a set of clusterings I and outputs a set of candidate clusterings eF. The crucial guarantee is that eF contains at least one fair clustering F that achieves a good approximation. The subroutine find-candidates assumes access to an α-close fair clustering algorithm. The candidate set eF is constructed as follows. For each clustering Ci ∈ I, we include its α-close fair clustering FCi in eF. In addition, for every ordered triple 

T = {C i, Cj , Ck} ∈ I × I × I,

we add to eF a clustering Ti,j,k obtained by applying the subroutine ClusterFitting to T .The pseudocodes of find-candidates and ClusterFitting are given in Algorithm 2 and Al-gorithm 3, respectively. 

Algorithm 2: find-candidates (I)

Input: A set I of clusterings 

Output: A set eF of candidate fair clusterings  

> 1

Initialize an empty set eF; 

> 2

for each Ci ∈ I do  

> 3

Fi ← a α-close fair clustering to Ci ; 

> 4

eF ← eF ∪ {F i} ; 

> 5

for each T = {C i, Cj , Ck} of I do

> 7

eF ← eF ∪ { Ti,j,k } 

> 8

return eF

Algorithm 3: ClusterFitting (T )

Input : Clusterings T = {C i, Cj , Ck}

Output: A fair clustering Ti,j,k  

> 1

V (G) ← V 

> 2

> 3

E−(G) ← { (a, b )|a, b ∈ V, a and b are separated in at least 2 clusterings } 

> 4

Ti,j,k ← a γ-approximation fair correlation clustering of G = ( V (G), E +(G) ∪ E−(G)) . 

> 5

return Ti,j,k 

Now we are ready to describe the algorithm algo-fair-con-stream . We work in the insertion-only stream described previously. The algorithm maintains two memory structures in parallel: 1. Sampled Store 1 (M1): Prior to the arrival of the stream, we independently sample 4g log m

134 indices j1, . . . , j 4g log m from [m] for some parameter g we fix the value of it later. For these sampled indices, all their corresponding triples that appear in the stream are stored in M1.2. Sampled Store 2 (M2): Similarly, before the stream starts, we sample 64 ε−2 log m indices 

k1, . . . , k t from [m], where t = 64 ε−2 log m and 0 < ε ≤ 1. For these sampled indices, all their triples are stored in M2 as they appear in the stream. After the stream ends. 1. We use union-find, to construct the clusterings Cj1 , . . . , Cjlog m from its triples stored in M1.Then we apply a subroutine find-candidates (Algorithm 2) on these ⌈log m⌉ clusterings to get a candidate set of fair clusterings eF. The subroutine find-candidates assumes access to a α-close fair clustering algorithm. 2. We use union-find, to construct the clusterings Ck1 , . . . , Ckt from its triples stored in M2. Let, 

W = {C k1 , . . . , Ckt }.We use W to find the best fair clustering F ∈ eF with respect to W, more specifically we find 

F = arg min  

> F′∈eF

X

> Ci∈W

dist( Ci, F′)

3. Return F.We provide the pseudocode of algo-fair-con-stream in the appendix.

Lemma 6.2.6. If there is a set T = {C i, Cj , Ck} such that, for all r̸ = s ∈ { i, j, k }, we have 

|Ir ∩ Is| ≤ ηAVG , then Obj ( I, T i,j,k ) ≤ (1 + 3( γ + 1) η)OPT .Proof. We define a set of bad pairs 

B = ( Ii ∩ Ij ) ∪ (Ij ∩ Ik) ∪ (Ik ∩ Ii),

that is, B contains all pairs of points (a, b ) such that a, b are together (resp. separated) in a majority of clusterings in T , while in F∗

> con

, a and b are separated (resp. together). Note that |B| ≤ 3ηAVG , as |Ir ∩ Is| ≤ ηAVG , for all r̸ = s ∈ { i, j, k }.Let G be a graph with vertex set V and the edge set E(G) = E+(G) ∪ E−(G), where 

E+(G) = {(a, b ) | a, b ∈ V, a and b are together in at least 2 clusterings among Ci, Cj , Ck} , and 

E−(G) = {(a, b ) | a, b ∈ V, a and b are separated in at least 2 clusterings among Ci, Cj , Ck} .

Observe that for each pair of vertices (a, b ), if (a, b ) /∈ B, then a, b are together (resp. separated) in F∗ 

> con

if (a, b ) is an edge in E+(G) (resp. E−(G)). On the other hand, if (a, b ) ∈ B, then a, b 

are separated (resp. together) if (a, b ) is an edge in E+(G) (resp. E−(G)). Therefore, if for every pair (a, b ) ∈ B, we flip their labels (from “ +” to “ −” and vice versa) we obtained a new graph G′

such that, any two vertices in a same cluster in F∗ 

> con

are connected by an “ +” edge, and any two vertices is different clusters in F∗ 

> con

are all “ −” edges. Hence, F∗

> con

6.2.2 Time and Space Complexity 

Now we analyse the time and space complexity of the algorithm algo-fair-con-stream .

Time Complexity. In M1 we store log m many clusterings. Hence, there are O(log 3 m) many candidate clusterings in eF. Computing a α-close fair clustering to each sampled clustering takes 

t2(n) log m time and running the procedure ClusterFitting on log 3 m triples of clusterings takes 

t1(n) log 3 m time. To find the best clustering in eF, we need to perform O(log 3 m) many checks. Now, in each check, we need to compute the distance between two clusterings, which takes O(n2) time, and since there are O(log m) clusterings in the W, the total time needed per check is O(n2 log m).Hence, overall for all checks, the time needed is O(n2 log 4 m). Hence, the overall time complexity is 

O(n2 log 4 m + t2(n) log m + t1(n) log 3 m)= O(n2 log 4 m + t1(n) log 3 m) (since t1(n) ≥ t2(n))

Space Complexity. In M1, we store information of ⌈log m⌉ many clusterings, hence we require a space of O(n log m) in M1. In M2 also, we store O(log m) clusterings; hence, in M2, too we need 

O(n log m) space. Hence, overall, the space complexity is O(n log m).

6.2.3 Proof of Main Theorem 

Now, we are ready to prove Theorem 11. To prove Theorem 11 we need to use Theorem 2. Recall Theorem 2 states that if there exists an algorithm to find α-close fair clustering to any given unfair clustering D, then given a correlation clustering instance G, there exists an algorithm to find an 

(α + ρ + αρ ) - approximate fair correlation clustering, where ρ is the state-of-the-art approximation factor for the correlation clustering problem. 141 Proof. From the previous section, we get that the time and space complexity of

.142 Chapter 7 

# Open Problems 

In this chapter, we highlight three compelling open directions that naturally arise from our study of closest fair clustering. These questions aim to deepen our understanding of fairness in clustering under different structural and algorithmic constraints. 

# 7.1 Improving the Approximation Factor for Multiple Colors 

In Theorem 6 and Theorem 7, we established algorithms for finding an α-close fair clustering where the approximation factor α depends polynomially on the number of color groups |χ|. While these results provide the first non-trivial guarantees for the multi-color setting, the dependence on |χ| can be undesirable—particularly when |χ| is large or comparable to the number of vertices |V |.An intriguing open direction is to design algorithms whose approximation factor is independent of |χ|, that is, to obtain a constant-factor approximation for the closest fair clustering problem. Achieving such a result would represent a significant conceptual and technical advancement, as it would eliminate the combinatorial dependence on the number of protected groups. Furthermore, as discussed in Chapter 5, a constant-factor approximation for the closest fair clustering problem would immediately imply constant-factor approximations for the fair correlation clustering and fair consensus clustering problems. This connection underscores the broader impact of improving the approximation factor in this setting. 

Question 7.1 . Given a clustering C on a vertex set V , where V is partitioned into disjoint color groups χ = {c1, . . . , c d}, can we design an algorithm that computes an O(1) -close fair clustering F

to C, independent of the number of colors d?

# 7.2 Extending to Alternative Fairness Notions 

Several alternative definitions of fairness have been explored in the clustering literature, each mo-tivated by different application scenarios and ethical considerations. One notable example is due to Ahmadian, Epasto, Kumar and Mahdian [AEK +20], where each color ci is associated with a parameter σi ∈ (0 , 1) . In their formulation, a clustering F is said to be fair if for every cluster 

F ∈ F we have ci(F ) ≤ σi|F | for all i, recall ci(F ) denotes the number of vertices of color ci in F .This definition allows for flexible upper bounds on group representation rather than enforcing strict proportionality, thereby offering a more permissive and context-sensitive notion of fairness. 143 We refer to such definitions collectively as relaxed notions of fairness . For a given clustering C,we define F∗C to be the closest relaxed fair clustering if 

dist( C, F∗C ) ≤ dist( C, F)

for all clusterings F that satisfy any relaxed fairness criterion. While our results in previous sections address proportional fairness, it remains an intriguing question whether similar approximation guarantees can be achieved for these relaxed notions. This leads to the following open problem. 

Question 7.2 . Given a clustering C on a vertex set V partitioned into disjoint color groups χ =

{c1, . . . , c d}, can we design an approximation algorithm to compute the closest relaxed fair clustering 

F to C?

# 7.3 Handling Overlapping Subgroups 

Fairness considerations in clustering often assume that the underlying demographic or protected groups form a disjoint partition of the dataset. However, in many real-world scenarios, individuals may belong to multiple groups simultaneously (for example, defined by intersections of gender, race, and age). This motivates the study of overlapping subgroup fairness , where the protected groups 

χ = {c1, . . . , c d} are not disjoint. Ahmadian and Negahbani [AN23] studied this setting in the context of the fair correlation clustering problem. Their fairness definition is same as Ahmadian, Epasto, Kumar, and Mah-dian [AEK +20], where each color ci is associated with a parameter σi ∈ (0 , 1) , and a clustering F

is said to be fair if for every cluster F ∈ F we have ci(F ) ≤ σi|F | for all i.Ahmadian and Negahbani provided a bicriteria constant-factor approximation for the fair cor-relation clustering problem under this definition. Here, “bicriteria” means that fairness constraints are allowed to be slightly violated in some clusters, i.e., 

ci(F ) ≤ (1 + ε) σi|F |

for some small ε ∈ (0 , 1) .A natural open question is whether similar bicriteria or exact guarantees can be achieved for the closest fair clustering problem under overlapping subgroup fairness. This would imply approx-imation for the fair consensus clustering problem. 

Question 7.3 . Given a clustering C on a vertex set V , where the color groups χ = {c1, . . . , c d} may overlap (i.e., each vertex may belong to multiple protected groups), can we develop an approximation algorithm to find the closest relaxed fair clustering F to C?144 145 Chapter 8

Let M1 ← empty store (hash from index → list of triples).  

> 4

Set t ← ⌈ ε−2 log m⌉ and draw K ← { k1, . . . , k t} ⊆ [m] uniformly at random.  

> 5

Let M2 ← empty store (hash from index → list of triples).  

> 6

Single-pass streaming phase:  

> 7

foreach triple (( u, v ), j, b ) arriving in the stream do  

> 8

if j ∈ J then  

> 9

append (( u, v ), b ) to M1[j] 

> 10

if j ∈ K then  

> 11

append (( u, v ), b ) to M2[j] 

> 12

Post-stream reconstruction:  

> 13

foreach j ∈ J do  

> 14

initialize a fresh union–find UF j on V 

> 15

foreach (( u, v ), b ) ∈ M1[j] do  

> 16

if b = 0 then  

> 17

union u and v in UF j 

> 18

Cj ← BuildClustersFromUF( UF j ) // extract clusters from union–find  

> 19

Let CJ ← {C j1 , . . . , Cjs }. 

> 20

Generate fair candidate set:  

> 21

eF ← find-candidates( CJ ) // uses α-close fair clustering algorithm  

> 22

Build W from M2: 

> 23

foreach k ∈ K do  

> 24

initialize a fresh union–find UF k on V 

> 25

foreach (( u, v ), b ) ∈ M2[k] do  

> 26

if b = 0 then  

> 27

union u and v in UF k 

> 28

Ck ← BuildClustersFromUF( UF k) 

> 29

W ← {C k1 , . . . , Ckt }. 

> 30

Select the best fair clustering on the sampled store 2: 

> 31

F ← ArgMin F′∈ eF

X

> Ci∈W

dist( Ci, F′) 

> 32

return F
